# El Arte de las Preguntas Poderosas (DIRECTOR) - PART 6

a tiny island with no natural \n\nresources except for a deep harbor and the collective talents of our 4.2 million people. That's it. It's miracle that we survived, and a greater miracle that we are thriving. We have a good, strong government. And yet, our progress has taken its toll. The very drive, authority, and unwavering focus that got us through the most difficult times are tough to shift now that we want to become a learning nation that's entrepreneurial, innovative, and creative. Singaporean poet Koh Buck Song sums this sentiment up in his poem "The Fragrance of Lallang" when he says, "But so strong is exertion's scent, it conquers all whiff of wonderment." \n\nAs a people, we often rely on "someone higher up" to solve our problems. Having worked in the government, I often won-dered, How can we build learning bridges between those in power and the other voices in our society so that something new can emerge? It's hard to make the transition from old ways to the new ways--even when people say they want to. I went to study abroad precisely to understand that, and to spend some time \n\nlooking at the world from different eyes. This question was still in my heart when I learned that the World Café had been intro-\n\nduced to Singapore by Daniel Kim, a co-founder of the MIT Sloan School's Organizational Learning Center, and his partner, Diane Cory. It's part of a larger effort to engage leaders from across the government and other key institutions in the principles and practices of learning organizations. When I told Juanita that I was going home for a month's visit, she asked if I'd like to explore how the World Café was being used in Singapore. The day before I left, Juanita e-mailed key people involved with Cafés to tell them that I was coming home and wanted to learn more about the World Café. \n\nAnd do you know what? By the time I touched down in Singapore there were already eager responses in my inbox--from the People's Association, the Police Department, the InfoComm Development Authority, the Housing Development Board, the Ministry of Man-power, and people working with local schools. They all wanted to meet and talk about their experiences with the World Café. I was amazed! Sheila Damodaran, of the Organizational \n\nSamantha Tan, a Research \n\nFellow at Harvard Univer-\n\nsity's Kennedy School of \n\nGovernment, is exploring \n\nleadership issues in educa-\n\ntion. After being away from \n\nSingapore for three years, \n\nshe returned home to visit \n\nwith family and to explore \n\nthe ways the World Café is \n\nbeing used as part of the Sin-\n\ngapore's mission to become \n\na "learning nation." Saman-\n\ntha's story demonstrates one \n\nof the ways in which the \n\nWorld Café is contributing to \n\nevolving cultures of dialogue \n\naround the world. \n\nS T O RY\n\nThe Call of Our Times 199 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nLearning Unit in the Singapore police force, not only attended most of my one-on-one meet-ings but also offered to organize a community Café among Singaporean World Café hosts and others from the Learning Organization Practitioners' Network, which she co-chairs. I'd like to share several vignettes of what I found on my Café learning journey. These aren't the only ones, but they will give you an idea of what's going on across our country. \n\nCreating Bridges Between Generations Eric Wee, a lecturer at the Temasek Polytechnic, teaches systems thinking to a group of sev-enteen-year-olds. He has them work in teams to apply systems thinking archetypes to issues like teenage pregnancy and youth smoking. He then invites policymakers and program fun-ders (for example, the National Youth Council, the Center for Fathering, and the Ministry of Health) to attend a Café with the students. Students, in teams of three, host Café tables on the various issues they've studied. Groups of adults move from table to table during each Café round. The students present their systems analysis of the issues--for example, why teenagers smoke. The adults ask questions and have very thoughtful conversations with the teenagers at the Café tables. In the past, students would typically be (a) less informed and more myopic about the issues, and (b) afraid of talking to adults. But in the Café conversa-tions the students have equal footing with adults. When people come together at the end of the Café, what emerges as a common theme is that parents and children really want to talk together more. The representative from the Center for Fathering is so inspired that he decides on the spot to initiate Café conversations between parents and children on a larger scale. \n\nGuns and Flowers Senior and junior staff on the police force are sitting together in a Café conversation--peo-ple from three or four different ranks, wearing their uniforms and carrying their guns, talk-ing together at those little tables with flower vases and checkered tablecloths. They are really listening and hearing each other's perspectives for the first time on how street officers are being affected, for example, by a new computerized tracking system that's been installed in police cars. After the Café, the senior officers say, "No matter how bright or smart our ideas are, we now realize we need ideas from the people at the ground level to make policies that actually work." And the juniors come to appreciate that the seniors aren't just authoritarian; they really are concerned about the welfare of the junior members. The police department decides to use the Café format for its annual corporate planning exercise, to create conversa-tions that include more voices in the process. \n\nNourishing the Community Through Dialogue The People's Association of Singapore, through the National Community Leadership Institute (NACLI), decides to merge the World Café with our local culture by creating P2P (People to People) conversations between government representatives and grassroots leaders using a cre-ative adaptation of the World Café called the Knowledge Kopitiam. Kopitiams are the traditional Singaporean neighborhood coffee shops that serve local specialties. They are places that local\n\n200 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\npeople have frequented since our early immigrant days to relax and talk informally about the issues of the day. NACLI launches its Knowl-edge Kopitiams by recreating a traditional kopitiam setting for its inaugural conversation, "Creating an Active Community in the New Economy." They call the kopitiam approach the "knowledge-traveling process." NACLI's magazine Kopitalk spreads the word about this "old/new" way of encouraging authentic conversation on key issues, and the kopitiam idea begins to spread. After experiencing the Knowl-edge Kopitiams at NACLI, Yaacob Ibrahim, then senior parliamentary \n\nsecretary for the Ministry of Communications and Information Technology, comments, "Today we want to spark a revolution, not to overthrow the government, but to reinvent our-selves. . . . These people-to-people or P2P discussions are essential to our development as a cohesive and well-informed people. I am pleased that NACLI has captured the relevance of such dialogues to reinvent the way grassroots forums can be organized." \n\nThe Café Travels The Knowledge Kopitiam concept begins to spread to other government and institutional settings. Members of the organizational learning community find they can host a Café with-out too much difficulty, tailoring it to address their own organization's important questions. The Ministry of Defense hosts Café dialogues to explore the question, "How can we expand our purpose from deterrence to nation building?" In line with one of its corporate objec-tives--to be a learning organization--the Housing Development Board introduces Café con-versations in its orientation program for new officers in order to create time and space for real conversation about their hopes, aspirations, and concerns. Schools convene Café dia-logues among a broad cross section of schoolteachers and administrators around the ques-tion, "Given the changing needs of our country, what does it mean to teach?" The InfoComm Development Authority and the Ministry of Manpower adopt the Knowledge Kopitiam to explore how internal departments can learn from each other and focus their collective efforts on nurturing a culture of creativity and innovation in Singapore. \n\nWhat Does It Mean to Care? For me, the most surprising Café discoveries came at the end of my visit, when Sheila Damodaran from the police department, along with Ivy Ooi and Anthony Lim from the Housing Development Board, convened a community gathering of World Café hosts and other members of the Learning Organization Practitioners' Network. I was amazed when close to seventy people showed up! We decided to put the question "What does it mean to care?" at the center of the Café. \n\nSeveral things happened that afternoon that showed me how the World Café is con-tributing to a culture of dialogue in Singapore--responding to our yearning and caring as a people for having real conversations across the boundaries of the hierarchies that so often \n\nThe Call of Our Times 201 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nseparate us. For example, there was a woman in the Café dressed very plainly who seemed out of place, although I knew she'd been invited. I'd describe her as a "tea lady"--a service person who is usually pretty invisible in our society. In the whole-group conversation at the end of the Café, she courageously stood up and said softly, but with much certainty, "You know, it's important to feel people care, because when people care, it makes life worth living." The room was struck silent by her piercing humanity. Her words spoke to a whole national issue--to our utilitarian mindset where people sometimes feel like widgets--and how debilitating and soul-destroying it is. I was moved. Everyone was moved. And in that room, I knew that policy deci-sions would shift because of her. That moment somehow changed me forever. \n\nI think one of the surprising learnings for everyone doing Café work in Singapore is that Café conversations are a constructive bridge between levels and between the past and the future. It's a national issue because we want to promote entrepreneurship, creativity, and innovation, and we're struggling to learn how to create environments that redefine the authority relationship. Yet to do this, we are partnering with the authorities! It's a paradox. \n\nWe like structure in Singapore. We've found that the World Café provides a clear struc-ture, but like cooks using a large mixing bowl, we can put in different ingredients and invent new dishes. I've also tried to understand why Café dialogues have worked so well in Singa-pore, an Asian culture with so many diverse ethnic groups. The Café provides an informal and relaxed environment that celebrates and harnesses difference yet also helps us see what connects us. Seeing this happen gave me an insight into the real meaning of empower-ment--power generated from the ground up by seeing new connections and building peer-to-peer relationships across the boundaries that so often divide us. \n\nCould the World Café represent a new form of activism--an activism of the spirit? It's not an activism against the authority structure, but for the world we want. It's a humanistic activism, because in the Café you are responding to a common question, but you are called to respond from wherever you sit in relation to the question. It is fundamentally a very respectful process--a form of pro-activism rather than re-activism. That's what I want to see in Singapore--people actively co-creating--helping each other through dialogue to be, as it were, in our dreams awake . . . collectively shaping our future. \n\nI've become very proud of the work that is happening in Singapore, especially because of the quality of heart that I now see exists here. I experienced that feeling in the many Singaporeans I met with who contributed to my being able to tell this story. I fell in love again with my coun-try. By seeing where and how Café conversations are spreading, I realized that in addition to our logical, rational, practical, and results-oriented mindset, our small nation also has passion, enthusiasm, and soul, and has a positive role to play in the direction that Asia and the world are moving. This idea of conversation--talking together, reaching mutual understanding, and mak-ing meaning together across hierarchies--that's the work that needs to be done. That's where the potential is. The World Café is bringing that in practical ways to our journey forward as a nation. \n\n. . .\n\nO \n\nn the evening before beginning to write these reflections \n\non creating a culture of dialogue, I participated in \n\na Café gathering of Latino and Anglo parents, teachers, \n\nschoolchildren, and local residents in the tiny community of Point \n\nReyes Station, California. It's located at the tip of Tomales Bay, an \n\nendangered pristine ecosystem just an hour and a half north of \n\nSan Francisco. This rural area is the home to a diverse popula-\n\ntion--oyster fishermen, cattle ranchers, organic produce and dairy \n\nfarmers, environmental activists, urban-techno refugees, artists and \n\nwriters--along with farmhands, nursery workers, gardeners, and \n\ntradespeople, many of whom come originally from Mexico. Inter-\n\nest groups often spar with each other. Local issues, including edu-\n\ncation, have at times become heated and acrimonious. \n\nDavid and I have lived in this area part-time for the last \n\ndecade, and we will likely spend our later years here. David had \n\nbeen asked by a small group of moms at the local West Marin \n\nSchool to host a World Café dialogue on the question, "What are \n\nthe elements of an ideal educational experience for young people \n\nin our community?" The gathering was, it seems, a first--a large \n\nbilingual, bicultural event in which all advertising and every \n\naspect of the meeting itself would be conveyed in both Spanish \n\nand English. The whole community, whether parents or not, was \n\ninvited to attend. And for the very first time, schoolchildren were \n\nincluded as equal partners in the conversation about the future of \n\ntheir own learning. \n\nOver one hundred community members turned out for the \n\ngathering, a mass meeting for our small community. As the Café \n\nunfolded, David and I were deeply moved by the unexpected con-\n\nnections being made. At one table, a Latino farmworker parent sat \n\nbeside a nine-year-old boy, his ponytailed dad, and a local con-\n\ntractor who had run for county supervisor. At another table was a \n\nteacher, a twelve-year-old girl, the coordinator of the neighbor-\n\nhood radio station, and an environmental activist. All were \n\n202 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations\n\ndeeply engrossed in thinking together about how, as a commu-\n\nnity, they could create the best learning for young people. \n\nIn the whole-group conversation at the end of the Café, \n\nmembers from very different worlds discovered that their hopes \n\nand dreams for the youth of the community were not so different \n\nfrom each other after all. People discovered that underneath what \n\nhad appeared as local controversy was a yearning for authentic \n\nconnection, personal relationships, good ideas, and committed \n\naction on behalf of the common good. Someone said, "I think it's \n\nimportant that we form a Parent-Teachers Association (PTA)." \n\nAnother popped up and asked, "Why couldn't it be Parent-\n\nTeacher-Student-Community Association?"--and the room broke \n\nout clapping with excitement at the thought of it. \n\nYou might ask, "Why were you so moved? What's the rele-\n\nvance of a single Café of a hundred people in a tiny northern \n\nCalifornia town for the 'big question' of creating a culture of dia-\n\nlogue in the larger world?" David and I believe that this small \n\nlocal story is an important reminder of the call of our times, the \n\ncall to actively engage across the imagined boundaries that so \n\noften separate us in order to address the challenges facing our \n\nfamilies, our organizations, our local communities, our national \n\nsocieties, and our global village. \n\nThe Call of Our Times \n\nLet's face it. The challenges we face are widespread and complex. \n\nThey threaten the very survival of our species and our fragile \n\nbiosphere. These "wake-up calls" include environ-\n\nmental degradation and climate change, unem-\n\nployment, poor education, urban violence, and \n\nother problems associated with the growing \n\nschism between rich and poor, both within and \n\nbetween nations. These systemic issues are exacerbated by a host \n\nof military conflagrations, the availability of weapons of mass \n\ndestruction, and the excesses of global economic competition. \n\nWe live with a growing awareness that important spiritual, eth-\n\nical, and ecological dimensions of life have been sorely neglected \n\nThe Call of Our Times 203 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe challenges we face are \n\nwidespread and complex\n\nand are now threatening to engulf us with unanticipated conse-\n\nquences. Perhaps most sobering is the rapidly escalating tendency \n\nto separate "us" from "them," to create chasms rather than bridges \n\nin the face of differences in religious or political beliefs, cultural \n\nvalues, and personal lifestyles. \n\nIt is becoming painfully clear that none of us in this vulnera-\n\nble and interconnected world can go it alone. No matter what the \n\nlevel of system--from our own families to our global commu-\n\nnity--we are being called to forge paths to the future that will \n\nenable us to use the dilemmas and diversity in our midst in order \n\nto generate more creative, holistic, and wiser responses to the \n\nchallenges we face. \n\nThe question for our times is how to do this. Philosopher \n\nJacob Needleman asks, "How can we come together and think and \n\nhear each other in order to touch, or be touched, by the \n\nintelligence we need? . . . We need each other to think \n\nwell about these questions. And therefore, we come \n\nround again to the question of how to structure our \n\nwork of thinking together" (1997, p. 4). Our \n\nresponse to Needleman's challenge is to remind our-\n\nselves and others that authentic conversation is our \n\nhuman way of thinking together. It has been since \n\nour earliest days, when our ancestors gathered around fires in \n\ncouncil to resolve differences and discover ways to confront dan-\n\ngers to their survival. \n\nCouncil was one of the earliest structures used to focus the \n\nwork of thinking together in conversation. The World Café stories \n\nin this book offer another intentional conversational structure for \n\nthinking together--one based on simple design principles that \n\ncan enhance our capacities to think more holistically, embrace \n\ndiverse perspectives, and create actionable knowledge across \n\ntraditional boundaries. Whether it be exploring the future of \n\neducation in a tiny California town, engaging a multistakeholder \n\nsustainability forum in Sweden, deliberating questions of peace \n\nand war at a Canadian law school, reaching across the divide \n\nbetween Israeli Arabs and Jews, including patients and physicians \n\n204 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAuthentic conversation \n\nis our human way of \n\nthinking together\n\nin a pharmaceutical company's strategy, improving school per-\n\nformance in Florida, reducing company safety risks at Hewlett-\n\nPackard, or bridging organizational hierarchies in Singapore, the \n\nWorld Café is serving as a useful conversational process and pat-\n\ntern for accessing and enlivening co-intelligent action. \n\nAn Era of Opportunity \n\nThankfully, as our need for thinking together effectively and \n\nconnecting together personally becomes more acute, creative \n\napproaches to dialogue and engagement are burgeoning. \n\nAppreciative Inquiry (Cooperrider and others, 2003), Open \n\nSpace (Owen, 1997), Future Search (Weisbord and Janoff, \n\n2000), and circle practice (Baldwin, 1994), along with other \n\napproaches to shaping the future (Bunker and Alban, 1997; \n\nHolman and Devane, 1999), are making unique contributions. \n\nInnovative face-to-face and computer-assisted approaches to \n\npublic deliberation and citizen engagement are on the rise \n\n(Atlee, 2003). Key initiatives like Conversation Cafés, Commons \n\nCafés, Let's Talk America, From the Four Directions, Public Con-\n\nversations Project, the Pioneers of Change, the National Coali-\n\ntion for Dialogue and Deliberation, and the Fetzer Institute's \n\nCollective Wisdom Initiative are highlighting dialogue processes \n\nthat are showing great promise for accessing the collective wis-\n\ndom that lies beyond traditional advocacy and argumentation-- \n\nin the public sphere, in education, health, government, and in \n\nthe corporate world. These exciting efforts are enabling many of \n\nus who have been adversarial activists in the past to experience a \n\ndeeper intelligence that is grounded in our understanding of \n\nsystems thinking and the interconnectedness of all life. Our dis-\n\ncoveries are leading us to the recognition that there's no "them" \n\nout there. We're becoming what Tom Atlee, the founder of the \n\nCo-Intelligence Institute, calls "social process activists," who \n\nlook beyond the adversarial stances that abound on both the \n\nright and the left. Process activism focuses on how we engage \n\ntogether around critical issues, not just what particular position \n\nwe are advocating. \n\nThe Call of Our Times 205 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\nSocial process activists seek to engage diverse perspectives as \n\nco-intelligent resources for the common good by employing a \n\nrange of dialogue and deliberation approaches for discovering the \n\ngreater wisdom of the whole. This type of proactive and interac-\n\ntive stance provides a clear contrast to our current culture of dia-\n\ntribe, which tends to demonize and demean the "other." \n\nAnother unexpected resource for creating an active culture of \n\ndialogue is also coming into play. The very communications tech-\n\nnologies that have in many ways separated us from the rhythms \n\nof the natural world and from relationships with each other are, \n\nsimultaneously, making our collective predicament visible in ways \n\nwe could never have previously imagined. Mass media, assisted by \n\nthe Internet and other Web-based technologies, now makes it \n\npossible for the whole world instantaneously to see young sol-\n\ndiers being killed and women or children being maimed in con-\n\nflicts half a planet away. \n\nAs humans we have the unique capacity for reflective aware-\n\nness--the capacity to step back together and ask, "Why is this \n\nhappening? Is there a better way?" In fact, the word consciousness \n\ncomes from con-scire, meaning "knowing together." Our capacity \n\nfor knowing together is now being honed through the use of \n\ndensely connected electronic networks that are catalyzing growing \n\nwebs of connected conversations about what we want for our \n\ncommon future--not only in local communities and organiza-\n\ntions but also in this global village that is our common place of \n\norigin. The World Café, as a similar pattern of networked conver-\n\nsations, is simply a smaller-scale version that we can use in order \n\nto engage our collective intelligence around the questions we \n\nmost care about in order to shape our futures more intentionally. \n\nAs Peter Russell has pointed out, our "global brain" is awak-\n\nening to its life-affirming potential (1995, 1998). Enabled by \n\nonline connections, citizen action networks and nongovernmen-\n\ntal organizations (NGOs) are springing up, helping millions of us \n\nreach out to each other, both locally and across the boundaries of \n\ngeography, religion, class, and culture in our search for innovative \n\npaths forward. In the corporate world, customers who are talking \n\n206 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nwith each other online about the quality of a company's ethics, \n\nproducts, and services are engendering a revolution in the ways \n\ncorporations engage key stakeholders. \n\nPerhaps Rick Levine and his co-authors of the best-seller The \n\nCluetrain Manifesto: The End of Business as Usual summarized it \n\nbest when they said: "The real point is that the Internet has made \n\nit possible for genuine human voices to be heard again. . . . There \n\nare millions of threads in this conversation, but at the beginning \n\nand end of each one is a human being. . . . It's not the end of the \n\nworld. It's the beginning of a new one" (Levine and others, 2000, \n\npp. xii, 32, 36, 37). \n\nAcross town, across sectors, and across the world, we now \n\nknow that, in the words of Cesar Chavez and the farmworkers' \n\nmovement of my youth, "Sí, se puede!"--"Yes, it can be done!" \n\nA Time for Choices \n\nHowever, as we look at today's challenges, the question remains: \n\nNow that we know what is possible, how will we evolve a global culture \n\nin which people--including our national and international leaders-- \n\nuse dialogue and deliberation rather than vindictiveness and violence as \n\nthe preferred way of dealing with differences and of \n\nliving together as a human community? The Chinese \n\ncharacter for crisis means both danger and oppor-\n\ntunity. We are living in such a moment, in which \n\nthe future hangs in the balance. There is no cer-\n\ntainty that just because we have the opportunity \n\nand the tools for constructive change we will \n\nchoose to use them wisely. \n\nThe work of biologist Humberto Maturana reminds us that \n\n"our human existence is one in which we can live in whatever \n\nworld we bring about in our conversations, even if it is a world \n\nthat finally destroys us as the kind of beings that we are" (Matu-\n\nrana and Verden-Zöller, n.d., p. 48). What if, as Maturana sug-\n\ngests, you knew that the meanings and choices you make each \n\nday in conversation with your spouse, your children, your friends, \n\nor your colleagues either contributed to the well-being of the \n\nlarger whole or contributed to its demise? What if others began to \n\nThe Call of Our Times 207 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWe can live in whatever \n\nworld we bring about in \n\nour conversations\n\nsee that, too? And what if you had access to simple tools, \n\nprocesses, and infrastructures that enabled you to host and \n\nconvene life-affirming conversations around your own--or your \n\norganization's or your community's--most important questions? \n\nAs we shared earlier, architect and philosopher Christopher \n\nAlexander has deeply influenced our thinking about how large-\n\nscale change occurs in human \n\nsocieties. He points out that liv-\n\ning systems, including human \n\nsystems, are made up of wholes \n\nat every level of scale--from the \n\nindividual, to the family, all the \n\nway to complex organizations, \n\ntowns, cities, and societies. He \n\nsuggests that life-enhancing \n\nimprovements actually co-evolve \n\nnot from grand plans or edicts \n\nfrom a central authority but \n\nfrom small acts of collaboration \n\nbased on a repetition of life-\n\naffirming patterns--like the fun-\n\ndamental pattern of engaging in \n\nconversations that matter--at \n\nevery level of scale. "Every act \n\nhelps to repair some larger, \n\nolder whole," Alexander \n\nexplains, "but the repair not \n\nonly patches it, it also modifies \n\nit, transforms it, sets it on the \n\nroad to becoming something \n\nelse, entirely new" (1979, p. 485). He shows how millions of \n\nthese tiny transformations, carried out locally in any living sys-\n\ntem, can, over time, spread their effects to transform the character \n\nof the system as a whole. \n\nPerhaps it is as simple as that. . . . Exercising our ability to \n\nrespond to the call of our times to foster more authentic and \n\n208 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nCo-Evolving Our Futures\n\ncourageous conversations in whatever sphere of influence we have \n\nbeen given. . . . Knowing that growing numbers of people around \n\nthe globe, in their own spheres of influence, are doing the same. . . . \n\nSensing that, at a deeper level, all of these conversations and \n\norganizational innovations are already connected to a powerful life \n\nforce that we as a species now have the opportunity to access \n\nintentionally on behalf of our common future. \n\nAn Invitation to Community \n\nI invite you to engage in an act of faith. I ask you to imagine that \n\nwe do have the power to make a difference through fostering con-\n\nversation, community, and committed action in our lives and work. \n\nI deeply believe that when we help to change the collective conver-\n\nsation about a situation, we have the opportunity to influence the \n\nfuture of that situation, whatever it may be and at whatever level of \n\nscale it occurs. Creating the conditions from which life-affirming \n\nfutures will emerge is not a spectator sport. It involves daily partici-\n\npation and practice, just like other activities we value. \n\nIn the Australian aboriginal culture there is a belief that there \n\nis one large song of life, and that each of us has our unique \n\n"songline" that contributes to the wholeness and beauty of that \n\nsong. It is in a spirit of contributing our songline to this larger \n\nsong of life that our work both with the World Café and with \n\nother forms of generative conversation finds its home. \n\nThere are many ways to participate. Experiment with Café \n\nconversations in your own life and work. Share your discoveries \n\nand questions with the World Café listserv where the World \n\nCafé's community of inquiry and practice gathers to learn \n\ntogether. Visit the World Café Web site to hear the latest Café \n\nadventures and the emerging "Voices of the World Café." Take a \n\nlook at "Resources and Connections" at the end of this book, as \n\nwell as the bibliography, to discover many additional doorways \n\nto dialogue and deliberation. We hope you will experiment with \n\nthose that seem most suited to the needs of your own situation \n\nand would love to hear from you about others that you think \n\nwe should let people know about. By hosting, convening, and \n\nThe Call of Our Times 209 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\nparticipating in courageous conversations about questions that \n\nmatter, we can, together, contribute to creating a culture of dia-\n\nlogue and developing the collective wisdom needed to create a \n\nlegacy of hope for future generations. \n\nI would like to end these reflections with a poem written by \n\nour dear friend, colleague, and World Café creative partner, Nancy \n\nMargulies. She expresses the spirit that I, as your host, hope you \n\nwill take with you from our journey together through these pages. \n\n210 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAt the World Café . . . \n\nAt the World Café, \n\ntrusting the strength \n\nthat lives in the space between us, \n\nfilled with images and thoughts \n\nthat give meaning to our lives. \n\nExperiencing \n\nyour words echoing in my thoughts, \n\nas questions finding their answers. \n\nNoticing \n\nthat it's always been this way, \n\nConversation enabling us to find meaning, \n\nconnecting across imagined boundaries. \n\nI give the gift of listening. \n\nAsking, I discover more than I knew. \n\nMeaning emerges from our shared \n\nsense of what is and what could be. \n\nBecoming \n\na larger Self that knows its capacity. \n\nTogether, we create a future worth living.\n\nWhat is one question or issue that you personally care deeply about in relation to your family, your work situation, your community, your church, or any other part of your life, that, if explored with others, could make a difference to the future of that situation? \n\nWho else might you invite to engage with you in exploring that question? \n\nHow might you use the Café conversation principles (whether or not you use a Café format) to support the quality of that conversation? \n\nWhat is the next step in your own learning about hosting and convening conversations that matter in your own spheres of influence? \n\nThe Call of Our Times 211 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection\n\n212 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWe are deeply grateful to Anne \n\nDosher, Ph.D., now in her \n\neighties, who has been a guide, \n\nmentor, and inspiration for the \n\nWorld Café and other related \n\ndialogue initiatives around the \n\nglobe. One of the developers of \n\nthe National Network for \n\nYouth, Anne received the Cali-\n\nfornia Woman-in-Government \n\nAward from the California leg-\n\nislative assembly in recogni-\n\ntion of her many years of \n\npublic service. She more \n\nrecently became a co-founder \n\nof the Institute for Relational \n\nDevelopment and elder of the \n\nAshland Institute. Here Anne \n\nshares the story of the ques-\n\ntions that have informed her \n\nown lifelong explorations in \n\nthe field of dialogue and its \n\npromise for our common \n\nfuture. \n\nE P I L O G U E \n\nHow Can We Talk It Through? By Anne W. Dosher \n\nAt a World Café event in 2002, David Isaacs introduced me as \n\nthe elder and "guardian of the soul" of the World Café. He \n\nasked why, toward the end of a long life, I was so engaged and \n\ncommitted to the World Café. Normally reticent, in that moment \n\nI found my memory spiraling back in time. Born on the northeast \n\ncoast of England, of Viking and Welsh ancestry, I grew up as a \n\ngranddaughter of the Empire and daughter of the Common-\n\nwealth. As a young girl in the years following the First World War, \n\nI puzzled and then grieved that the men came back so hurt--legs \n\nlost, difficulty breathing--and that so many were dead. How could \n\nwe have done this? I wondered. Why couldn't we have talked it through? \n\nLater, studying the armistice conditions ending the war, it \n\nbecame very clear that the lack of ongoing and authentic dialogue \n\namong nations created conditions for future conflict. I determined \n\nthat, when I grew up, I would study ways in which these mistakes \n\nwould not be repeated. But then, instead of peace, World War II \n\ncame, and I spent almost five years in the Royal Air Force. The over-\n\narching mission was simple: survive, defeat Nazism, end holocausts, \n\nand make the world safe for democracy. In the course of wartime, I \n\nlost friends, comrades, and home. One searing experience in Europe, \n\nin which I encountered ambulatory Jews being brought out of the \n\ncamps, caused me to ask my commanding officer: "Sir, how could we \n\nhave done this?" He snapped that, of course, we had not done this, \n\nthey had. Yet I knew that somehow our human community as a \n\nwhole had failed in the face of these atrocities. \n\nI married an American soldier, and in 1946 my life in the \n\nUnited States began. I have served my adopted country as a com-\n\nmunity psychologist for almost sixty years, including the develop-\n\nment of services for youth and communities at risk. To that end, \n\nI've worked with local, state, and federal governments to develop \n\nequitable laws, sufficient funding, and innovative programs. As I\n\nEpilogue 213 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nsat with alienated youth in barrios and ghettoes, on beaches and \n\nreservations, the questions continued to haunt me: How could we \n\nhave done this? Why couldn't we have talked it through? \n\nIn my contemplation and study of these questions, it became \n\nclear to me that every societal change process I knew of started \n\nwith an informal conversation in which men and women--young \n\nor old--were witnessed and "heard into speech," sharing their \n\ndreams and hopes for making a difference around something they \n\ncared about. In being truly seen and heard, people were trans-\n\nformed and discovered their mutual commitment to act. That \n\nsmall group then went on to invite other groups into the conver-\n\nsation and the change became more and more real. \n\nAs a community psychologist, I used this understanding to \n\ndesign social networks for healing and transforming community \n\nand societal systems. I saw that these small circles of conversation \n\nwere becoming the birthing place of a new human awareness. \n\nThese networks of small circles of conversation springing up \n\naround the world were bringing forth an emerging collective con-\n\nsciousness that yearned for a more equitable and sustainable \n\nfuture for all people on this planet. After years of quietly living \n\nthe insight about the relationship between dialogue circles and \n\nthe evolution of larger-scale social movements, I encountered the \n\nWorld Café network. Immediately I recognized in Café conversa-\n\ntions a clear metaphor for what life itself had taught me. \n\nSince the day the Café was born in Juanita and David's living \n\nroom, I have recognized its value and have been intimately \n\ninvolved in guiding its evolution. We make it a point to gather \n\nregularly with other World Café pioneers to share our experi-\n\nments and learnings. The tragedy of September 11 occurred just \n\nbefore one of these gatherings was due to open in Sweden. In the \n\nwake of the collective grief, fear, and confusion, there was some \n\nquestion as to whether we should still go. Our Swedish colleague, \n\nBo Gyllenpalm, decided it for us when he said, "Of course, you'll \n\ncome. If you don't come, they win." \n\nI remember feeling that we were meant to be there to harvest \n\nwhat the global pioneers of the World Café were discovering. Our\n\n214 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nvital question--How can we talk it through?--resonated even more \n\nprofoundly and deeply under those circumstances. At our meeting \n\nin Sweden, I think the whole group felt the same sense of urgency \n\nto refine what we were all learning together so that our work \n\ncould be a force for peace during these troubled times. \n\nHow can we talk it through? The question that has informed all \n\nof my life choices, now lies, I believe, at the heart of our capacity to \n\nsurvive as a species and to ensure that our home, this beautiful \n\nplanet, survives. For me, it is the core question that informs all of \n\nour other questions. If we could converse and talk things out, we \n\nwould find new ways of being together in the world. But instead, \n\nwe separate, conflicts begin, and when worse comes to worse, we go \n\nto war. Then death comes in as a walking partner, instead of life. \n\nI get so sad when I see how we adults cut off the conversa-\n\ntions our children have with life--with grass, trees, birds, and \n\ntheir own imaginations. Engagement in the conversation of life is \n\ndiscouraged when in fact it is the most essential of our capacities, \n\nit is embracing a spirit of true conversation that connects us to all \n\nforms of life. We cannot have a true conversation when we're not \n\nopen to the other, no matter what form the other takes. Often \n\npeople don't know how to be open, to reach out to others. \n\nAuthentic conversation without openness is not possible. I want \n\nto suggest to practitioners, to emerging leaders, and to our chil-\n\ndren, that life itself can be a conversation--with other people, \n\nwith nature, and with all of life. \n\nHow can we talk it through? When we talk \n\ntogether, we have a choice between destructive \n\ndiatribe and constructive dialogue. We can \n\nchoose life and connection or separation and dis-\n\nintegration. We have the opportunity to choose \n\nlife, but to do so, conversation is essential. Listen-\n\ning to each other and truly talking together--these are deep, \n\nsociospiritual actions. People think talking is not action. That's a \n\nmistake. Conversation is a profound action that helps us to \n\nexpand our consciousness and connect together parts and people \n\nWe have the opportunity \n\nto choose life, but to do \n\nso, conversation is essential\n\nEpilogue 215 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nthat are separated. I can't think of anything else that does that. It \n\nis one of our unique human paths to fulfillment and wholeness. \n\nIt is we, collectively, who can make the agreement as to how \n\nwe will live together, and that only happens when we talk about \n\nit. We can't do it alone. We need to look at the \n\ndilemmas of our current situation and ask, "How \n\ncould we have done this?" We need to look together \n\nat what has happened to humanity because we \n\ncouldn't talk it through! \n\nI have been part of many experiments in \n\nexploring the question: How can we talk it through? \n\nThe promise of the World Café and other dialogue \n\napproaches is that we now know that we can expe-\n\nrience a collective intelligence and wisdom in rela-\n\ntion to complex questions. We can, by stepping in \n\nand becoming conscious participants in the con-\n\nversations of our lives, increase the probability of the right \n\nchoices being made--life-affirming choices. Life itself is asking us \n\nto become co-evolutionists--to be responsible for our common \n\ndestiny--to reconnect with the natural world and with each other. \n\nI believe that making the choice to participate in this way is actu-\n\nally a sacred act. I define sacred as that which has value in and of \n\nitself and gives meaning to the whole. \n\nThe stories in this book flow into a great new story, inviting \n\nhumanity to learn to speak honestly with one another, to listen \n\ntruly to one another, and to listen intently together for what is \n\nemerging in our midst. The stories of the World Café invite us to \n\nsearch for questions that open gateways to co-create the future, \n\nto make visible new collective thoughts as they emerge, and to \n\ndiscover creative ways of living and being together. I invite you \n\nto find your place at a small Café table in the "world as Café," \n\nknowing that you are already connected and interdependent with \n\nthis larger whole. I ask you to have the confidence that each \n\nwholehearted conversation you are part of can make a difference \n\nto our common future. \n\nIt is we, collectively, who \n\ncan make the agreement \n\nas to how we will live \n\ntogether, and that only \n\nhappens when we talk \n\nabout it\n\n216 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThis is quite a long answer to the question David asked me in \n\n2002, and yet I think you can see that, as elder and guardian of the \n\nsoul of the World Café, I embrace this work as sacred and view life \n\nitself as a conversation. When I see the care and love of Café hosts \n\nsetting out tables, placing the small flowers in the center, attending \n\nto the precision of questions that matter, and welcoming people at \n\nthe doorway, I cherish the knowledge that our human souls and the \n\nsoul of the world are being honored. \n\nNow, with gratitude to our ancestors and blessings to all of \n\nlife, I invite you to enter the gateway to the future, discovering \n\nyour own best hopes along with honoring the needs of future \n\ngenerations, including the young and those not yet born. \n\nWelcome! Find a seat with people you haven't met. I invite you \n\ninto conversations that serve life, and bless your efforts to dis-\n\ncover how we can talk it through--together.\n\n217 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nA F T E R W O R D \n\nDiscovering the Magic of Collective Creativity By Peter M. Senge \n\nAll my life I have been drawn to the puzzle of collective creat-\n\ning. How is it that sometimes, as if by magic, people create some-\n\nthing together that has beauty, power, and life? A sports team that \n\nsuddenly moves to another plateau where the game is no longer \n\nthe game but a vehicle for an aesthetic statement (and yet the \n\ngame is still there). A symphony orchestra that disappears into \n\nthe music. A dance troupe that ceases being individual dancers. \n\nAn individual racer who joyously hugs her "opponents" at the \n\nend of a race, knowing that it was only from their collective \n\nstriving that her performance was possible. \n\nThis question has drawn me and guided me, but it has wor-\n\nried me as well. Do the recurring examples of collective creating \n\nonly happen in sporting events or in the performing arts? Is this \n\nwhy singing, dancing, drumming, running, and jumping have \n\nbound human cultures together for all our history? But these \n\nactivities are less central in modern cultures, and as adults we are \n\nmore likely to encounter them as spectators than as participants. \n\nInstead, our lives revolve around teaching, managing, engineer-\n\ning, parenting, doctoring, and coping with countless daily \n\nstresses. As one of many who has argued that collective creating \n\ncan be cultivated in our daily working lives, I worry if the case has \n\nbeen overstated. While the possibility is undeniable, the practices \n\nfor reliably realizing that possibility have remained elusive. \n\nThis is why the World Café has drawn me. Café conversations \n\nare the most reliable way I have yet encountered for all of us to \n\ntap into collective creating. The stories in this book provide ample \n\nillustration. They bring back many memories for me, from when \n\nJuanita and I first started experimenting with using World Cafés \n\nin countless different gatherings over the past decade--small and \n\nlarge, east and west, north and south. \n\nPeter Senge is a senior lecturer \n\nat the Massachusetts Institute \n\nof Technology and the found-\n\ning chair of the Society for \n\nOrganizational Learning (SoL). \n\nAuthor of the widely acclaimed \n\nbook The Fifth Discipline, \n\nwhich introduced dialogue as a \n\nkey to organizational learning, \n\nPeter shares his decade of \n\nexperience with the World Café \n\nand its contribution to collec-\n\ntive creativity. \n\nThroughout all of these experiences, I have been repeatedly \n\nstruck by the ease of beginning a World Café-style dialogue-- \n\nhow readily people shift into heartfelt and engaging conversa-\n\ntions. No formal instruction is needed to start, unlike most \n\norganizational development or group techniques. This suggests \n\nsomething important. The World Café is not a \n\ntechnique. It is an invitation into a way of being \n\nwith one another that is already part of our nature. \n\nI have been struck also by the power and \n\nimpact of many World Café conversations. At the \n\nend of a three-day gathering of fourteen executives \n\nengaged in Café dialogues, Arie de Geus, one of the \n\npioneers of organizational learning, said simply, "I \n\nam always amazed at what can arise from a collec-\n\ntive learning process." As importantly, I have been \n\nstruck by the elegance and simplicity of the whole process and \n\nconsequently by its transferability. I can think of very few \n\nprocesses for thinking together that work equally well in executive \n\nretreats, annual corporate budget and planning meetings, one-\n\nthousand-person conferences, and community gatherings where \n\nstrangers come together to consider the environment we create for \n\nour kids to grow. \n\nThe World Café is not only a reliable method for tapping into \n\ncollective creating. It is also a powerful metaphor for transforming \n\nhow we think about work in general and why organizations suc-\n\nceed or fail as vehicles for collective creating in particular. What if \n\nwe thought of teams of people working together--either formal or infor-\n\nmal teams--as being like individual "table conversations" in a larger \n\nCafé? What if we thought about each such team interacting with \n\nother teams as being like what happens when team members \n\nrotate from one Café table to another, influencing and being \n\ninfluenced by each other through the web of conversations in \n\nwhich they are participating. \n\nFollowing this line of imagination, it is not difficult to envi-\n\nsion an organization as a living network of people and groups "in \n\nconversation." This is happening all around us, already. But it is \n\n218 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe World Café is not a \n\ntechnique. It is an invita-\n\ntion into a way of being \n\nwith one another that is \n\nalready part of our nature\n\nmostly happening much less powerfully than it might. Although \n\ninterwoven conversations of people interacting are indeed occur-\n\nring, these interactions do not necessarily carry the power and \n\nenergy that they could. Indeed, this one distinction differentiates \n\nthe vast number of mediocre organizations from the few great \n\nones. It also suggests why organizations that are great for a while \n\nlater fade. In short, do the conversations that define the organization \n\nsucceed or fail in generating creative energy? \n\nThe answer to this question lies, I believe, less in the character \n\nor talents of the individuals involved than in the quality of the \n\nquestions that sit at the heart of their conversations. A Café that \n\nfails to center on questions that have real heart and meaning \n\nbecomes a mechanical process of people talking and moving and \n\nreporting back. It fails to generate energy and excitement for the \n\nsame reason that most organizations fail to generate energy--the \n\nquestions and issues with which people are engaging simply do \n\nnot compel their commitment and imagination. \n\nHaving said that, it is curious how rarely this happens in \n\nactual Café conversations. Why? Could it be that, when left on \n\ntheir own in a conducive environment, people naturally gravitate \n\ntoward questions that matter? That they naturally do not waste \n\ntheir time on things that are unimportant? Perhaps authentic \n\nconversation often fails to occur in organizations because we \n\nbelieve we lack the permission to focus on what truly matters to \n\nus, or because we are actually kept from doing so? Whatever the \n\ncombination of internal and external forces, the nature of the \n\nproblem is clear. Life is too short to waste time on anything but \n\nwhat is truly important--and we all know this. \n\nThe biologist Humberto Maturana, who has been a major \n\ninfluence on how many of us think about communication and \n\nhuman communities, says, "History follows the path of our \n\ndesires." When I first heard Humberto say this, I was confused. \n\nIt seemed to me that much of the history I have seen, including \n\nrecent history, follows the path of no one's desire. Indeed, it seems \n\nto be dominated by unanticipated and undesired side effects of \n\nour actions--climate change that no one desires, growing stress \n\nAfterword 219 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\n220 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nbetween rich and poor that no one desires, growing insecurity that \n\nno one desires. \n\nAs I have pondered this statement I have come to realize that \n\nHumberto is inviting us into a stance of responsibility that we \n\nusually shirk. Although the consequence of our col-\n\nlective actions may not be what we seek, the motiva-\n\ntions that give rise to these actions, and thus the \n\nconsequences, are expressions of our desires. But \n\nthese desires are too small. They are too self-cen-\n\ntered. They arise in ways too disconnected from the \n\ndesires of others. In short, the desires guiding history \n\ntoday are not the desires that can create a future big \n\nenough for all of us. \n\nI believe the underlying purpose of the World \n\nCafé is to let loose the true desires of the larger \n\nwhole. Is it too big a stretch of our imaginations to envision a \n\nworld engaged in conversations that have real heart and meaning \n\nfor us all? Start hosting your own Café conversations and see \n\nfor yourself. \n\nIs it too big a stretch \n\nof our imaginations to \n\nenvision a world engaged \n\nin conversations that \n\nhave real heart and \n\nmeaning for us all?\n\n221 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nA C K N O W L E D G M E N T S \n\nIt Takes a Village to Raise a Child \n\nThere is an ancient African proverb that tells us, "It takes a \n\nvillage to raise a child." Watching the World Café grow and \n\nevolve over more than a decade has been humbling and deeply \n\nrewarding for both David and me. We often sit with wonder and \n\nask ourselves, "Who could have imagined . . . !" We feel honored \n\nand blessed by the love, competence, and commitment of all of \n\nthose who have contributed to this work finding its expression \n\nacross the globe. The World Café community of inquiry and prac-\n\ntice is the true author of this book . . . we are but hosts to our \n\nmutual learning. The stories and reflections shared are simply \n\nillustrative of the many that could have found expression here. \n\nDavid and I offer our deepest gratitude to all those who have \n\nparticipated in and contributed to this journey and to this book-- \n\nmany more of you than we can adequately thank by name. Please \n\nknow that you have all been part of the village that is raising \n\nthis child. \n\nLeif Edvinsson and the Intellectual Capital Pioneers: Without your presence, participation, and excitement, the World Café would never have come into being. \n\nCharles Savage: Who first introduced us to the power of cross-pollinating people and ideas as part of the Café pattern language. large-scale systems change at Hewlett-Packard. We are \n\ngrateful to Sherrin Bennett, Susan Kelly, Jennifer Landau, and \n\nNancy Margulies for contributing key conceptual illustrations, to \n\nJames "Max" Maxwell, for his creative renderings throughout the \n\nbook, to Kevin Woodson for his beautiful chapter openers, and to \n\nLaura Lind for her elegant book design. Here's how to be in touch \n\nwith these artists as well as with other visual specialists. Their \n\ncontributions appear on the pages listed. \n\nSherrin Bennett, Interactive Learning Systems: Sherrin maps the dynamics of complex adaptive systems. As a consultant to organiza-tions, she helps people rise to meet a challenge with breakthrough thinking and innovative strategy. Using questions to clarify intent, appreciation to discover what works, and feedback to amplify improvements, her clients learn to co-create the future they want to see happen. Contact: sherrinbennett@earthlink.net. See pages 19, 37, 40, 48, 93, 104, 113, 114, 127, 128, 134, and 174. \n\nSusan Kelly, Occasions by Design: Susan is an independent graphic artist, specializing in visual thinking and interactive group graphics as well as agenda, meeting space, and materials design. For the past twenty years Susan has worked nationally and internationally, creating stimulating environments that bring out the creativity in individuals and groups. Contact: Susankellylistens@mac.com. See pages 35 and 182. \n\nJennifer Hammond Landau: As one of the pioneers of graphic facili-tation, Jennifer has provided visual process consulting and training to business, government, and community organizations since the early 1980s. Her specialty is "metaphor mapping," where information is presented and meetings facilitated with large, visual metaphors. Contact: jhlandau@earthlink.net. See pages 35 and 133.\n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . Illustration Credits 227 \n\nLaura Lind, Laura Lind Design: An award-winning graphic designer, Laura has twenty years' experience. She has designed many books and other printed materials, specializing in well-crafted visual communications and reliable service. You can see more of her work at lauralinddesign.com. \n\nNancy Margulies, Mindscapes: Part of the World Café team since the early years, Nancy is an organizational consultant, creative artist, illustrator, and inventor of Mindscaping, an innovative form of graphic recording. Using her unique combination of visual and strategic dialogue skills, Nancy's clients have included Xerox, Hewlett-Packard, Meg Wheatley, and President Bill Clinton and his cabinet. Contact: nm@montara.com. See pages 5, 63, 80, 83, 119, and 208. \n\nJames "Max" Maxwell: Max served as sketch artist for NBC-TV and won an Emmy Award for his work in documentaries. Max writes, paints, sculpts, and illustrates books, magazines, and other publications. Contact: www.mcn.org/b/jamesmaxwell. See pages 4, 22-23, 49, 64, and 167. In addition, Max either rendered or enhanced for publication every image in the book. \n\nKevin Woodson, partner, Visual Ink, LLC: For the past fifteen years Kevin has been combining art, facilitation, and strategic thinking to create business illustrations with Fortune 500 companies, including Hewlett-Packard, Gap Inc., and Citigroup. Kevin uses his artistic gifts to tell the story of change, make the complex simple, and get people onboard and excited. Contact: www.visualink.biz. See pages 13, 27, 43, 61, 79, 95, 107, 123, 139, 155, 177, and 197. \n\nVisual practitioners and graphic recorders: Visual practitioners can graphically record the whole of a conversation as it unfolds, enabling members to think more holistically and carry forward their insights more effectively because the "memory of the whole" is harvested and shared. You can find resources for graphic recording of World Café dialogues and other meetings at www. visualpractitioner.org, or by contacting inquiry@theworldcafe.com.\n\n228 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nADDITIONAL CREDITS \n\nPage 11: Photo of people at a Café table used with permission of the Shambhala Institute. \n\nPage 45: Artwork used with permission of FONAES. \n\nPage 72: Photo of Cafe´in airplane hanger used with permission of Saudi Aramco. \n\nPage 85: Photo of Café tablecloth and markers used with permission of Sherrin Bennett. \n\nPage 93: Questions for Reflection are adapted from "Questions and Ways of Being to Amplify Dialogic Possibilities: Stance and Focus of Attention." If you would like to learn how \n\nto participate in the growing online World Café community of \n\ninquiry and practice, visit the World Café Web site. There, World \n\nCafé hosts are sharing their learnings and discoveries. \n\nMaterials for Purchase \n\nPegasus Communications distributes World Café materials, \n\nincluding resource guides, key articles from its newsletter The \n\nSystems Thinker, a pocket guide, my doctoral dissertation on the \n\nWorld Cafe, and other dialogue resources. You can find Pegasus \n\nCommunications on the Web at http://www.pegasuscom.com. \n\nResearch and Internships \n\nI conducted the first doctoral research on the World Café (Brown, \n\n2001). We encourage other researchers and students to consider \n\nthe World Café for their research and internship projects. \n\nContact Dr. Fred Steier or Dr. Bo Gyllenpalm via e-mail at \n\ninfo@theworldcafe.com, if you'd like more information on this. \n\nNonprofit Foundation--Tax-Deductible Contributions \n\nProceeds from this book will be donated to the World Café \n\nCommunity Foundation, a nonprofit foundation whose mission \n\nis to develop and disseminate World Café and other innovative \n\ndialogue approaches for addressing key organizational and \n\nsocietal questions in the service of positive futures. You can \n\ncontribute to this work by making a tax-deductible donation to \n\nthe World Café Community Foundation, P.O. Box 783, Mill \n\nValley, California 94941. For more information, send an e-mail to \n\ninfo@theworldcafe.com or call 415/339-8714. \n\n229\n\n230 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe Co-Intelligence Institute http://www.co-intelligence.org Tom Atlee has compiled one of the Web's richest and most informative collections on the people, processes, and Web sites that foster dialogue and deliberation, democracy, and action. Of particular interest are the pages on dialogue and the compilations of processes. \n\nThe Fetzer Institute http://www.fetzer.org http://www.collectivewisdominitiative.org The Fetzer Institute is a private foundation that supports research, education, and service programs exploring the integral relationships among body, mind, and spirit. Its Collective Wisdom Initiative has been a leader in mapping the field of dialogue and collective intelligence. \n\nThe National Coalition for Dialogue and Deliberation (NCDD) http://www.thataway.org/ NCDD is a broad coalition of organizations and individuals involved in dialogue, public deliberation, and civic engagement. It is one of the most extensive resources for those interested in learning about dialogue and deliberation methods, practitioners, events, perspectives, and processes. \n\nAmerica Speaks http://www.americaspeaks.org America Speaks is a national not-for-profit organiza-tion committed to fostering civic dialogue and engaging citizen voices in local, regional, and national governance. \n\nThe Appreciative Inquiry Commons http://connection.cwru.edu/ai/ Appreciative Inquiry is an approach to positive change based on powerful questions and collaborative conver-sations about the best in people, their organizations, and the world around them. \n\nThe Berkana Institute http://www.berkana.org The Berkana Institute is a global charitable foundation founded by Margaret Wheatley and her colleagues. It supports life-affirming leadership around the world. Dialogue is a key component of the institute's work. \n\nThe Breakthrough Group http://www.thebreakthroughgroup.net The Breakthrough Group is a pioneer in engaging the art and the science of storytelling, including theater, simulation, and living case studies, to stimulate cat-alytic conversations and organizational learning. Breakthrough collaborates with the World Café to create innovative strategic dialogues in conferences and other organizational settings. \n\nThe Center for Nonviolent Communication (CNCV) http://www.cnvc.org CNCV is a global organization helping people connect compassionately with themselves and one another through Nonviolent Communication, a dialogue and feedback process created by Marshall B. Rosenberg, Ph.D. \n\nRELATED RESOURCES \n\nThis section provides information on other forms of dialogue as well as on key dialogue \n\ninitiatives that are also making wonderful contributions to this body of work. If you \n\nonly have limited time, the first three Web sites listed here provide a treasure trove of \n\ninformation, leading you into the many-faceted field of dialogue, deliberation, and col-\n\nlective intelligence. Those listed after these first three highlight specific aspects of this \n\nemerging field. We'd love to hear about other sites that you have found useful. Please \n\ncontact us at info@theworldcafe.com.\n\nResources and Connections 231 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe Center for Wise Democracy http://www.tobe.net The Center for Wise Democracy uses its unique Dynamic Facilitation process to help groups engage in dialogue and deliberation, especially through ran-domly selected Wisdom Councils of citizens through which the voice of "we the people" can be expressed in organizations and communities. \n\nThe Commonway Institute http://www.commonway.org/cafes The Commonway Institute hosts Commonway Cafés in varied venues, bringing people together across racial, political, and other traditional divides to encounter "the other" in ways that can transform our collective future. Commonway Cafés, Conversation Cafés, and the World Café have joined in a Café Collaborative, using common principles to nurture a culture of dialogue. \n\nThe Compassionate Listening Project http://www.compassionatelistening.org The Compassionate Listening Project, formerly Mid-East Citizen Diplomacy, is dedicated to empowering individuals to heal polarization and build bridges between people, communities, and nations in conflict through dialogue and heartfelt mutual listening. \n\nConversation Cafés http://www.conversationcafe.org Conversation Cafés are dialogues hosted in cafés and other public venues for meaningful conversations about our thoughts, feelings, and actions in these new times. Using a highly effective circle process, Conversation Cafés and the World Café have part-nered in linking small-group and large-group dia-logue approaches. \n\nCoVision http://www.covision.com CoVision was one of the first organizations to introduce portable groupware systems designed to facilitate the engagement of large numbers of stake-holders in meetings involving collaborative thinking and planning. CoVision's "Council" technology enables large groups to engage in collaborative dialogue through supporting quick keypad feedback on the group's priorities and key ideas. \n\nFran Peavey and Crabgrass http://www.crabgrass.org Fran Peavey, founder of Crabgrass, an NGO working on social and environmental issues, is a pioneer in the art of strategic questioning, particularly in community settings. She and her colleagues offer periodic train-ings in strategic questioning. \n\nFrom the Four Directions http://www.fromthefourdirections.org From the Four Directions is a global leadership initiative of the Berkana Institute. It has hosted and convened conversation circles among leaders in more than thirty countries. \n\nGlobal Renaissance Alliance (GRA) http://www.renaissancealliance.org Dedicated to furthering the work of peacemakers everywhere, the GRA has extensive conversational resources for convening Peace Circles. \n\nThe Heartland Institute http://www.thoughtleadergathering.com Using both circle and World Café dialogue processes, the Heartland Institute, a leader in the dialogue field, sponsors a series of Thought Leader Gatherings co-hosted by leading-edge thinkers. \n\nThe Institute of Noetic Sciences (IONS) http://www.noetic.org IONS is a global nonprofit organization with more than thirty-five thousand members. It conducts and sponsors research on the potentials and powers of consciousness. Its community groups have embraced both dialogue and the World Café process as effective learning and community group development models. \n\nInteraction Associates (IA) http://www.interactionassociates.com IA is a pioneer in meeting and facilitation training for those who need basic group skills as a foundation for working with dialogue and other group processes. \n\nThe International Forum of Visual Practitioners http://www.visualpractitioner.org/ Visual practitioners can graphically record the whole of a conversation as it unfolds, enabling members to think more holistically and carry forward their insights more effectively because the "memory of the whole" is harvested and shared. Those who work as visual practitioners include graphic recorders, conceptual illustrators, facilitators, artists, designers, and others. \n\n232 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nLet's Talk America http://www.letstalkamerica.org The goal of Let's Talk America is to engage Americans from across the political spectrum in respectful, thoughtful dialogue around questions that matter to the future of our democracy. Online resources include conversational guides for small- and large-group gatherings. \n\nThe Mediators Foundation http://www.mediatorsfoundation.org The Mediators Foundation focuses on developing projects and leaders that use dialogue as a core process for creating bridges among conflicting constituencies. \n\nNew Stories http://www.newstories.org New Stories is an organization that supports healthy individuals and communities, linked globally through dialogue, to create new stories for our common future. \n\nPeerSpirit http://www.peerspirit.com The core of PeerSpirit's work is "council," a powerful circle-based process that builds on the dialogue traditions of indigenous peoples. \n\nPioneers of Change http://www.pioneersofchange.net Pioneers of Change is an emerging, self-organizing, global learning community of committed young changemakers in their mid-twenties to early thirties from diverse cultural, social, and professional backgrounds. The network operates in over seventy countries and utilizes leading-edge dialogue and other group processes to support projects locally and globally. \n\nPowerful Non-Defensive Communication http://www.pndc.com Powerful Non-Defensive Communication is an approach to interpersonal dialogue that replaces the traditional mode of communicating to win arguments with one that gives each person the ability to communicate effectively without engaging in a power struggle. \n\nThe Public Conversations Project (PCP) http://www.publicconversations.org PCP promotes constructive conversations among people who have differing values, worldviews, and perspectives about divisive public issues. It offers many online resources. \n\nThe Socrates Café http://www.philosopher.org The Socrates Café encourages and supports lively dialogue in cafés, schools, and other venues among people who are curious and perplexed about the big questions of our time. \n\nSociety for Organizational Learning (SoL) http://www.solonline.org Founded by Peter Senge and other researchers, practi-tioners, and consultants, this group has sponsored pioneering research on dialogue and is a leader in the field of organizational learning. \n\nSpeaking Circles http://www.speakingcircles.com Speaking Circles is a process for transforming the way people communicate, allowing them to more fully express their natural creativity, passion, and humor, personally and professionally. \n\nThe Study Circles Resource Center http://www.studycircles.org Based on the Scandinavian study circle approach, the Study Circles Resource Center is dedicated to finding ways for people to engage in citizen dialogue and problem-solving on critical social and political issues. \n\nWisdom Circles http://www.wisdomcircle.org A wisdom circle is a way for small groups of people to create a safe space for dialogue in which to be trusting, authentic, caring, and open to change. \n\nWorld Café (Denmark) http://www.worldcafe.dk This site, hosted by Danish World Café pioneer, Finn Voldtofte, contains excellent information on Café hosting, conceptual pieces on dialogue and strategy, and leading edge thinking about "the magic in the middle."\n\n233 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAdams, M. See emergence World Bank, 45 World Café pioneers, 14-16, 34, \n\n213-14 \n\nZ Zohar, Danah, 104, 122\n\nA B O U T T H E A U T H O R S \n\nJuanita Brown, Ph.D., with her partner, David Isaacs, is a co-originator of the World Café. She collaborates as a thinking partner and design adviser with senior leaders, creating and hosting innovative forums for strategic dialogue on critical organizational and societal issues. Juanita's keynotes and seminars have attracted a broad range of leaders across sectors, including Fortune 100 compa-nies and members of government, health, education, and commu-nity-based organizations in the United States, Latin America, Europe, and the Pacific Rim. As a Senior Affiliate with the MIT Sloan School's Organizational Learning Center (now the Society for Organizational Learning), Juanita participated as a member of the core research team with the MIT Dialogue Project and as co-faculty for SoL's Executive Champions' Program. She has served as a Research Affiliate with the Institute for the Future and is a Fellow of the World Business Academy. \n\nDavid Isaacs is the president of Clearing Communications, an organizational and communications strategy company working with senior executives in the United States and abroad. He has col-laborated with a wide range of corporate clients, including Alpha-Graphics, Hewlett-Packard, Intel, Kraft General Foods, Scandinavian Airlines Systems, and Sanofi-Aventis. David's not-for-profit work has included hosting World Café dialogues with the Institute for Noetic Sciences, Shambhala Institute, Society for Organizational Learning, and Alliance for Transforming the Lives of Children. David has served as adjunct faculty at the California Institute for Integral Studies, St. Mary's College of California, and the University of Texas at San Antonio Business School's executive MBA program. \n\nContact information: Partners in life and work, Juanita and David make their home on Tomales Bay, California, a pristine ecosystem north of San Francisco. They can be reached at inquiry@theworldcafe.com or at 415/383-0129. \n\nThe World Café community is made up of organizational leaders and others who believe in the power of "conversations that matter" to help shape the future. Leaders from business, government, schools and universities, health systems, and community-based organizations around the world have contributed their stories and discoveries, both to this book and to our mutual learning. There are now skilled Café designers and hosts available in many locations. To learn more, contact inquiry@theworldcafe.com or call 415/383-0129. \n\n242 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nBerrett-Koehler is an independent publisher of books and \n\nother publications at the leading edge of new thinking and \n\ninnovative practice on work, business, management, leadership, \n\nstewardship, career development, human resources, entrepre-\n\nneurship, and global sustainability. \n\nSince the company's founding in 1992, we have been committed \n\nto creating a world that works for all by publishing books that \n\nhelp us to integrate our values with our work and work lives, and \n\nto create more humane and effective organizations. \n\nWe have chosen to focus on the areas of work, business, and \n\norganizations, because these are central elements in many \n\npeople's lives today.
